                        cas_cit16R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">16</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">First-principles study of alkali-metal intercalation in disordered carbon anode materials</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Huang, Jian-Xing; Csanyi, Gabor; Zhao, Jin-Bao; Cheng, Jun; Deringer, Volker L.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Materials Chemistry A: Materials for Energy and Sustainability</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Graphite and non-graphitising ("hard") carbons are important anode materials for battery technologies.  The electrochem. intercalation of alkali metals in graphite has been widely studied by first-principles d.-functional theory (DFT).  However, similar investigations of disordered "hard" and nanoporous carbons have been challenging due to the structural complexity involved.  Here, we combine DFT with machine-learning (ML) methods to study the intercalation of alkali metal (Li, Na, K) atoms in model carbon systems over a range of densities and degrees of disorder.  We use a stochastic approach to compute voltage-filling profiles, studying the three metal species side-by-side, and we analyze the ionic charges of metal atoms as a function of filling.  Our study provides at.-scale insight into the intercalation of all three alkali metals that are relevant to batteries, and it thereby makes a key step towards the DFT/ML-driven modeling of energy materials.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref17"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref17" class="refNumLink">17</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit17" data-doi="10.1149/1.1394081"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Stevens, D.         </div><div class="casAbstract">An electrochem. cell with beryllium X-ray windows has been designed and used for in situ small-angle X-ray-scattering studies of operating electrodes for the first time. The mechanism of electrochem. lithium and sodium insertion in nanoporous carbonaceous materials has been the subject of some recent controversy, which is resolved by the studies reported here.         </div><div class="casAbstract">The methods currently used to calc. at. pair distribution functions (PDFs) from org. structural models do not distinguish between the intramol. and intermol. distances.  Owing to the stiff bonding between atoms within a mol., the PDF peaks arising from intramol. atom-atom distances are much sharper than those of the intermol. atom-atom distances.  This work introduces a simple approach to calc.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Hard carbons are among the most promising materials for alkali-ion metal anodes.  These materials have a highly complex structure and understanding the metal storage and migration within these structures is of utmost importance for the development of next-generation battery technologies.  The effect of different carbon structural motifs on Li, Na, and K storage and diffusion are probed using d. functional theory based on exptl. characterizations of hard carbon samples.  Two carbon structural models-the planar graphitic layer model and the cylindrical pore model-are constructed guided by small-angle X-ray scattering and transmission electron microscopy characterization.  The planar graphitic layers with interlayer distance &lt;6.5 Å are beneficial for metal storage, but do not have significant contribution to rapid metal diffusion.  Fast diffusion is shown to take place in planar graphitic layers with interlayer distance &gt;6.5 Å, when the graphitic layer sepn. becomes so wide that there is negligible interaction between the two graphitic layers.  The cylindrical pore model, reflecting the curved morphol., does not increase metal storage, but significantly lowers the metal migration barriers.  Hence, the curved carbon morphologies are shown to have great importance for battery cycling.  These findings provide an at.-scale picture of the metal storage and diffusion in these materials.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref29"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref29" class="refNumLink">29</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit29" data-doi="10.1039/C8NR10383F"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Olsson, E.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chai, G.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Dove, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Cai, Q.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Adsorption and Migration of Alkali Metals (Li, Na, and K) on Pristine and Defective Graphene Surfaces</span>. <i>Nanoscale</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2019</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>11</i></span> (<span class="NLM_issue">12</span>),  <span class="NLM_fpage">5274</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">5284</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

        </div><div class="casAbstract">In this paper, a computational study of Li, Na, and K adsorption and migration on pristine and defective graphene surfaces is conducted to gain insight into the metal storage and mobility in carbon-based anodes for alkali metal batteries.  Atomic level studies of the metal adsorption and migration on the graphene surface can help address the challenges faced in the development of novel alkali metal battery technologies, as these systems act as convenient proxies of the cryst. carbon surface in carbon-based materials including graphite, hard carbons and graphene.  The adsorption of Li and K ions on the pristine graphene surface is shown to be more energetically favorable than Na adsorption.  A collection of defects expected to be found in carbonaceous materials are investigated in terms of metal storage and mobility, with N- and O-contg. defects found to be the dominant defects on these carbon surfaces.  Metal adsorption and migration at the defect sites show that defect sites tend to act as metal trapping sites, and metal diffusion around the defects is hindered when compared to the pristine surface.  We identify a defect where two C sites are substituted with O and one C site with N as the dominant surface defect, and find that this defect is detrimental to metal migration and hence the battery cycling performance.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref30"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref30" class="refNumLink">30</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit30" data-doi="10.5796/electrochemistry.85.391"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yamamoto, T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yamaguchi, T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Nohira, T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hagiwara, R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Fukunaga, A.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sakai, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Nitta, K.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Structural and Electrochemical Properties of Hard Carbon Negative Electrodes for Sodium Secondary Batteries Using the Na[FSA]-[C31pyrr][FSA] Ionic Liquid Electrolyte</span>. <i>Electrochemistry</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2017</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>85</i></span> (<span class="NLM_issue">7</span>),  <span class="NLM_fpage">391</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">396</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

Such characterization represents an important step toward understanding and improving the properties and functionality of porous carbons, yet few exptl. techniques have been developed for this purpose.  Here we demonstrate the application of NMR spectroscopy and pair distribution function (PDF) anal. as new tools to probe the local structures of porous carbons, alongside more conventional Raman spectroscopy.  Together, the PDFs and the Raman spectra allow the local chem. bonding to be probed, with the bonding becoming more ordered for carbide-derived carbons (CDCs) synthesized at higher temps.                         cas_cit33R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">33</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Expansion of the carbon-carbon bond length in potassium graphites</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Nixon, David E.; Parry, G. S.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Physics, C., Proceedings of the Physical Society, Solid State Physics</span>

    </div><div class="casAbstract">Studies of the x-ray diffraction pattern from different K graphites have shown that intercalation of graphite by K is accompanied by an expansion of the C layers.  This expansion is uniform within a single layer and is const. for all n C layers sepg. successive intercalate layers in the stage n compd. of compn. C12nK (n&gt;1).  Preferred values of the C-C bond length for stages 1 to 6, derived from the value of the a-axis parameter extrapolated to θ = 90°, are: Stage 1 1.43204 (3) A., Stage 2 1.42592 (3) A., Stage 3 1.4240 (1) A., Stage 4 1.42305 (7) A., Stage 5 1.42267 (6) A., and Stage 6 1.4221 (1) A.  Lower accuracy in Stages 3 and 6 is due to slight variation between samples, attributed to deviations from the ideal stoichiometry.  Before intercalation, the C-C bond length of the pyrolytic graphite was 1.42114 (3) A.  The bond-length expansion is consistent with electron transfer from K to the antibonding orbitals in the upper π-band of the graphitic region between successive intercalate layers.  The discontinuous change of bond length with stage can be approx. represented by the relation: C-C (Stage n) = (1.4203 + 0.0113/n) A.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref34"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref34" class="refNumLink">34</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit34" data-doi="10.1021/acs.chemmater.8b01390"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Bommier, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ji, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Greaney, P.                         cas_cit34R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">34</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Electrochemical Properties and Theoretical Capacity for Sodium Storage in Hard Carbon: Insights from First Principles Calculations</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Bommier, Clement; Ji, Xiulei; Greaney, P. Alex</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemistry of Materials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2019</span>),

        </div><div class="casAbstract">This paper uses DFT calcns. to explore amorphous carbon material, in its context as a NIB anode.  Simulations are 1st used to validate the exptl. detd. storage mechanism, with those results then being extrapolated to elucidate a theor. capacity limit.  Through studying a breadth of structures, with multiple Na configurations, the studies leads to four major conclusions.  First, the nature of Na storage in carbon materials changes with increasing Na concn. in a continuum from ionic storage to metallic plating.  Secondly, the authors reveal the crit. role of the intersheet spacing, stacking misalignment, and effects of spacing expansion on the feasibility of Na intercalation into graphitic structures.  This leads to the 3rd and 4th conclusion, which stipulates the results provided here offer compelling support towards an earlier exptl. derived Na ion storage for hard carbon materials, along with the existence of a theor. limit of sodium ion storage in hard carbon materials.  Also, the techniques and breadth of work involved are highly relevant to future simulations exploring amorphous carbon as an active material, whether it should be for LIB anodes, supercapacitors, or catalysts.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref35"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref35" class="refNumLink">35</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit35" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201602894"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Bommier, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chong, Z.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A comparative study of the electrode/electrolyte interface was carried out for lithium and sodium metal anodes in electrolytes consisting in 1 M LiPF6 in EC0.5:DMC0.5 (LP30) and 1 M NaPF6 in both EC0.5:DMC0.5 and EC0.45PC0.45DMC0.1.  Sym. Li/Li cells exhibited low polarization and smooth charge discharge curves with current densities of 0.1 and 1 mA/cm2.  In contrast, large overpotentials were obsd. even at 0.1 mA/cm2 for Na/Na cells.  Such differences cannot be related to ionic cond. of the electrolytes but are rather due to an enhanced interfacial resistance (Rct + RSEI) as deduced from impedance measurements.  The compn. of the SEI layer was investigated by FTIR and found to be stable for Li electrodes but to evolve upon cycling for Na electrodes which is also in agreement with differences in surface morphol. detected by SEM.  A lower stability (partial soly.) of the SEI would also enable to understand the differences in the impedance of identical hard carbon (HC) electrodes in cells with either Li or Na counterelectrodes.                         cas_cit40R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">40</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Commercialisation of high energy density sodium-ion batteries: Faradion's journey and outlook</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Rudola, Ashish; Rennie, Anthony J. R.; Heap, Richard; Meysami, Seyyed Shayan; Lowbridge, Alex; Mazzali, Francesco; Sayers, Ruth; Wright, Christopher J.; Barker, Jerry</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Materials Chemistry A: Materials for Energy and Sustainability</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2021</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">9</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">13</span>),

        </div><div class="casAbstract">There is no doubt that rechargeable batteries will play a huge role in the future of the world.  Sodium-ion (Na-ion) batteries might be the ideal middle-ground between high performance delivered by the modern lithium-ion (Li-ion) battery, desire for low costs and long-term sustainability.  To commercialise the Na-ion technol., Faradion was founded in 2011 as the world's first non-aq. Na-ion battery company.  Over the years, we have made rapid progress in increasing the all-around performance of Na-ion batteries, benefitting from decades' worth of industry experience and prior Na-ion as well as Li-ion academic research.  The Faradion Na-ion chem. can now exceed the energy densities of LiFePO4//graphite Li-ion batteries with rapidly converging cycle lives, similar rate performance and charge acceptance.  In addn., our technol. makes use of lower materials costs, offers improved safety through the use of high flash point electrolytes and has the ability to be discharged to zero volts for storage and transportation.  In this article, Faradion's step-by-step progress in the Na-ion technol. will be discussed together with a general picture of how our Na-ion chem. compares with other Na-ion systems and com. available Li-ion technol.  Finally, the importance of starting exptl. testing on new materials, keeping com.-relevant protocols in mind, will be illustrated by clearly highlighting the drastic effects of some crucial exptl. factors.  By sharing such industry know-how, Faradion hopes researchers worldwide will adopt such exptl. protocols as routine methodol. in the lab.  These simple measures can significantly shorten the path from a new invention to com. application, while also ensuring that the battery-related literature conveys the true com. feasibility of an invention or discovery to the general public.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref41"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref41" class="refNumLink">41</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit41" data-doi="10.1107/S1600577515002283"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Basham, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Filik, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wharmby, M.                         cas_cit41R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">41</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Data Analysis WorkbeNch (DAWN)</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Basham Mark; Filik Jacob; Wharmby Michael T; Chang Peter C Y; El Kassaby Baha; Gerring Matthew; Aishima Jun; Levik Karl; Pulford Bill C A; Sikharulidze Irakli; Sneddon Duncan; Webber Matthew; Dhesi Sarnjeet S; Maccherozzi Francesco; Ashton Alun W; Svensson Olof; Brockhauser Sandor; Naray Gabor</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of synchrotron radiation</span>

    </div><div class="casAbstract">Synchrotron light source facilities worldwide generate terabytes of data in numerous incompatible data formats from a wide range of experiment types.  The Data Analysis WorkbeNch (DAWN) was developed to address the challenge of providing a single visualization and analysis platform for data from any synchrotron experiment (including single-crystal and powder diffraction, tomography and spectroscopy), whilst also being sufficiently extensible for new specific use case analysis environments to be incorporated (e.g.  ARPES, PEEM).  In this work, the history and current state of DAWN are presented, with two case studies to demonstrate specific functionality.  The first is an example of a data processing and reduction problem using the generic tools, whilst the second shows how these tools can be targeted to a specific scientific area.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref42"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref42" class="refNumLink">42</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit42" data-doi="10.1107/S0021889804011744"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Qiu, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Thompson, J. P.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Automatic Tuning Matching Cycler (ATMC) in Situ NMR Spectroscopy as a Novel Approach for Real-Time Investigations of Li- and Na-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>J.                 10.1016/j.jmr.2016.02.008</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D265%26publication_year%3D2016%26pages%3D200-209%26journal%3DJ.%2BMagn.%2BReson.%26author%3DO.%2BPecher%26author%3DP.%2BM.%2BBayley%26author%3DH.%2BLiu%26author%3DZ.%2BLiu%26author%3DN.%2BM.%2BTrease%26author%3DC.%2BP.%2BGrey%26title%3DAutomatic%2BTuning%2BMatching%2BCycler%2B%2528ATMC%2529%2Bin%2BSitu%2BNMR%2BSpectroscopy%2Bas%2Ba%2BNovel%2BApproach%2Bfor%2BReal-Time%2BInvestigations%2Bof%2BLi-%2Band%2BNa-Ion%2BBatteries%26doi%3D10.1016%252Fj.jmr.2016.02.008&amp;doi=10.1021%2Fjacs.1c06058&amp;doiOfLink=10.1016%2Fj.jmr.2016.02.008&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit44R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">44</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Automatic Tuning Matching Cycler (ATMC) in situ NMR spectroscopy as a novel approach for real-time investigations of Li- and Na-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Pecher, Oliver; Bayley, Paul M.; Liu, Hao; Liu, Zigeng; Trease, Nicole M.; Grey, Clare P.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Magnetic Resonance</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2016</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">265</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">200-209</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JMARF3</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1090-7807</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier B.V.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">We have developed and explored the use of a new Automatic Tuning Matching Cycler (ATMC) in situ NMR probe system to track the formation of intermediate phases and investigate electrolyte decompn. during electrochem. cycling of Li- and Na-ion batteries (LIBs and NIBs).  The new approach addresses many of the issues arising during in situ NMR, e.g., significantly different shifts of the multi-component samples, changing sample conditions (such as the magnetic susceptibility and cond.) during cycling, signal broadening due to paramagnetism as well as interferences between the NMR and external cycler circuit that might impair the expts.  We provide practical insight into how to conduct ATMC in situ NMR expts. and discuss applications of the methodol. to LiFePO4 (LFP) and Na3V2(PO4)2F3 cathodes as well as Na metal anodes.  Automatic frequency sweep 7Li in situ NMR reveals significant changes of the strongly paramagnetic broadened LFP line shape in agreement with the structural changes due to delithiation.  Addnl., 31P in situ NMR shows a full sepn. of the electrolyte and cathode NMR signals and is a key feature for a deeper understanding of the processes occurring during charge/discharge on the local at. scale of NMR. 31P in situ NMR with "on-the-fly" re-calibrated, varying carrier frequencies on Na3V2(PO4)2F3 as a cathode in a NIB enabled the detection of different P signals within a huge frequency range of 4000 ppm.  The expts. show a significant shift and changes in the no. as well as intensities of 31P signals during desodiationc/sodiation of the cathode.  The in situ expts. reveal changes of local P environments that in part have not been seen in ex situ NMR investigations.  Furthermore, we applied ATMC 23Na in situ NMR on sym. Na-Na cells during galvanostatic plating.  An automatic adjustment of the NMR carrier frequency during the in situ expt. ensured on-resonance conditions for the Na metal and electrolyte peak, resp.  Thus, interleaved measurements with different optimal NMR set-ups for the metal and electrolyte, resp., became possible.  This allowed the formation of different Na metal species as well as a quantification of electrolyte consumption during the electrochem. expt. to be monitored.  The new approach is likely to benefit a further understanding of Na-ion battery chemistries.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref45"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref45" class="refNumLink">45</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit45" data-doi="10.1006/jmre.2002.2554"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Harris, R. class="rightTab-fig internalNav"></a><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. PDF (<i>G</i>(<i>r</i>)) data for the hard carbons used in this study</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/jacs.1c06058/asset/images/large/ja1c06058_0001.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/jacs.1c06058&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><figure data-id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><a class="internalNav" aria-label="scroll to figure" href="#fig2"><img src="/cms/10.1021/jacs.1c06058/asset/images/medium/ja1c06058_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/jacs.1c06058/asset/images/large/ja1c06058_0002.jpeg" id="rightTab-gr2" class="rightTab-fig internalNav"></a><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. (a) A schematic of the structural parameters included in the model. The parameters in red (fragment size, <i>x</i>, <i>y</i>, and number of layers, <i>n</i>) are not directly refineable, but are instead optimized by changing their values and rerunning the fit. Parameters in blue are refined during the fit. An overall scale factor, and the delta<sub>1</sub> peak sharpening parameter, are also included in the fit but not shown in the schematic. Fits of a stacked curved graphene sheet model to (b) the PDF and (c) the reduced structure function, <i>F</i>(<i>Q</i>), of the 1100 °C sample (<i>R</i><sub>w</sub> = 0.16).</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/jacs.1c06058/asset/images/large/ja1c06058_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/jacs.1c06058&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><figure data-id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><a class="internalNav" aria-label="scroll to figure" href="#fig3"><img src="/cms/10.1021/jacs.1c06058/asset/images/medium/ja1c06058_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/jacs.1c06058/asset/images/large/ja1c06058_0003.jpeg" id="rightTab-gr3" class="rightTab-fig internalNav"></a><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3.             (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Hard carbon possesses the ability to store Li, Na, and K ions between stacked sp2 carbon layers and voids (micropores).  We have explored hard carbon as a candidate for neg. electrode materials for Li-ion, Na-ion, and K-ion batteries.  Hard carbon samples have been prepd. by carbonizing sucrose at different heat treatment temps. (HTTs) in the range of 700-2000°C to make them structurally suitable for reversible Li, Na, and K insertion.  Structures and particle morphol. of the hard carbon samples synthesized at different HTTs were systematically characterized using X-ray diffraction, small-angle X-ray scattering, pair distribution function anal., electron microscopy, Raman spectroscopy, and ESR spectroscopy.  All these characterizations of hard carbon samples have revealed advanced ordering of carbons and redn. of carbon defects with increasing HTT.  Thus, the av. stacked carbon interlayer distance decreases, the no. of the stacking layers increases, the layered domains grow in the in-plane direction, and interstitial voids enlarge.  Electrochem. properties of the hard carbons were examd. in nonaq. Li, Na, and K cells.  Potential profiles and reversible capacities upon galvanostatic charge/discharge processes in nonaq. cells are significantly different depending on HTTs and different alkali metal ions.  On the basis of these findings, strategies to design high-capacity hard carbon neg. electrodes for high-energy-d. Li-ion, Na-ion, and K-ion batteries are discussed.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref5"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref5" class="rightTabRefNumLink">5</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit5" data-doi="10.1039/D0EE01363C"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Au, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Alptekin, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Jensen, A.                         rightTab-cas_cit5R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">5</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">A revised mechanistic model for sodium insertion in hard carbons</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Au, Heather; Alptekin, Hande; Jensen, Anders C. S.; Olsson, Emilia; O'Keefe, Christopher A.; Smith, Thomas; Crespo-Ribadeneyra, Maria; Headen, Thomas F.; Grey, Clare P.; Cai, Qiong; Drew, Alan J.; Titirici, Maria-Magdalena</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Energy &amp; Environmental Science</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2020</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">13</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">10</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">3469-3479</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">EESNBY</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1754-5706</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Hard carbons have shown considerable promise as anodes for emerging sodium-ion battery technologies.  Current understanding of sodium-storage behavior in hard carbons attributes capacity to filling of graphitic interlayers and pores, and adsorption at defects, although there is still considerable debate regarding the voltages at which these mechanisms occur.  Here, ex situ23Na solid-state NMR and total scattering studies on a systematically tuned series of hard carbons revealed the formation of increasingly metallic sodium clusters in direct correlation to the growing pore size, occurring only in samples which exhibited a low voltage plateau.  Combining exptl. results with DFT calcns., we propose a revised mechanistic model in which sodium ions store first simultaneously and continuously at defects, within interlayers and on pore surfaces.  Once these higher energy binding sites are filled, pore filling occurs during the plateau region, where the densely confined sodium takes on a greater degree of metallicity.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref6"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref6" class="rightTabRefNumLink">6</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit6" data-doi="10.1021/acs.nanolett.5b01969"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Bommier, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Surta, T. W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Dolgos, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ji, X.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">New Mechanistic Insights on Na-Ion Storage in Nongraphitizable Carbon</span>. <i>Nano Lett.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2015</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>15</i></span> (<span class="NLM_issue">9</span>),  <span class="NLM_fpage">5888</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">5892</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1021/acs.nanolett.5b01969</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit6R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">6</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">New Mechanistic Insights on Na-Ion Storage in Nongraphitizable Carbon</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Bommier, Clement; Surta, Todd Wesley; Dolgos, Michelle; Ji, Xiulei</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nano Letters</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2015</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">15</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">9</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">5888-5892</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">NALEFD</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1530-6984</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Nongraphitizable carbon, also known as hard carbon, is considered one of the most promising anodes for the emerging Na-ion batteries.  The current mechanistic understanding of Na-ion storage in hard carbon is based on the "card-house" model first raised in the early 2000s.  This model describes that Na-ion insertion occurs first through intercalation between graphene sheets in turbostratic nanodomains, followed by Na filling of the pores in the carbon structure.  We tried to test this model by tuning the sizes of turbostratic nanodomains but revealed a correlation between the structural defects and Na-ion storage.  Based on our exptl. data, we propose an alternative perspective for sodiation of hard carbon that consists of Na-ion storage at defect sites, by intercalation and last via pore-filling.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref7"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref7" class="rightTabRefNumLink">7</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit7" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201901351"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sun, N.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Guan, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Cao, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhu, Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, P.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xu, B.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Extended “Adsorption–Insertion” Model: A New Insight into the Sodium Storage Mechanism of Hard Carbons</span>. <i>Adv.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Hard carbon (HC) is one of the most promising anode materials for sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) due to its suitable potential and high reversible capacity.  At the same time, the correlation between carbon local structure and sodium-ion storage behavior is not clearly understood.  In this paper, the two series of HC materials with perfect spherical morphol. and tailored microstructures were designed and successfully produced using resorcinol formaldehyde (RF) resin as precursor.  Via hydrothermal self-assembly and controlled pyrolysis, RF is a flexible precursor for high-purity carbon with a wide range of local-structure variation.  Using these processes, one series of five representative RF-based HC nanospheres with varying degrees of graphitization were obtained from an RF precursor at different carbonization temps.  The other series of HC materials with various microscopic carbon layer lengths and shapes was achieved by carbonizing five RF precursors with different crosslinking degrees at a single carbonization condition (1300 °C and 2 h).             (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Successfully enabling a new battery technol., such as sodium-ion batteries, requires a thorough understanding of the functional properties of its building blocks.  Knowing how the electrode materials behave upon operation is crucial to gain insight into how the battery technologies can be improved for optimal usage.  Here we examine with X-ray total scattering and subsequent pair distribution function (PDF) anal. the structural response of hard carbon upon operation, which has been proposed as a possible electrode material for sodium ion batteries.  PDF anal. reveals a clear correlation between the interplane and in-plane interat. distances and the state of charge.  The change in in-plane graphene behavior corresponds to a reversible charge transfer between sodium and the antibonding orbitals in the upper π band of the graphene sheet, resulting in in-plane elongation and contraction upon cycling.  As a result of the introduction of sodium into the structure upon discharge, the hard carbon structure is found to become increasingly disordered resulting in the initial structure not being able to fully recover upon desodiation.  The more pronounced structural impact upon sodiation than seen in lithiated hard carbon suggests a larger electron transfer impact on the structure by influencing the π-orbitals of the neighboring, conjugated benzene rings.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium ion batteries (SIBs) using hard carbon as neg. electrode hold the promise of being low cost alternative to lithium ion batteries (LiBs). The current work employs N2, Kr and CO2 gases to correctly assess the changes in hard carbon porosity induced by different pyrolysis temp. of cellulose.  The sloping capacity was found to decrease with the decrease of the specific area of ultramicropores measurable only by CO2 adsorption, while the plateau capacity demonstrated an opposite behavior.  The high temp. derived carbons (&gt; 1400 °C) present no porosity which disqualifies the attribution of plateau region to the adsorption of Na+ in the nanopores but rather the insertion between the pseudo-graphitic domains. The agreement of exptl. results and computational findings yields a clear picture of the Na insertion mechanism, which can be described by four different stages that are dominated by surface morphol., defect concn., bulk structure, and nanoporosity.  On the basis of the resulting model for sodium insertion, design strategies are suggested to maximize the capacity of hard carbon.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref16"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref16" class="rightTabRefNumLink">16</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit16" data-doi="10.1039/C9TA05453G"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Huang, J.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Graphite and non-graphitising ("hard") carbons are important anode materials for battery technologies.  The electrochem. intercalation of alkali metals in graphite has been widely studied by first-principles d.-functional theory (DFT).  However, similar investigations of disordered "hard" and nanoporous carbons have been challenging due to the structural complexity involved.  Here, we combine DFT with machine-learning (ML) methods to study the intercalation of alkali metal (Li, Na, K) atoms in model carbon systems over a range of densities and degrees of disorder.  We use a stochastic approach to compute voltage-filling profiles, studying the three metal species side-by-side, and we analyze the ionic charges of metal atoms as a function of filling.         </div><div class="casAbstract">The methods currently used to calc. at. pair distribution functions (PDFs) from org. structural models do not distinguish between the intramol. and intermol. distances.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Hard carbons are among the most promising materials for alkali-ion metal anodes.  These materials have a highly complex structure and understanding the metal storage and migration within these structures is of utmost importance for the development of next-generation battery technologies.  The effect of different carbon structural motifs on Li, Na, and K storage and diffusion are probed using d. functional theory based on exptl. characterizations of hard carbon samples.  Two carbon structural models-the planar graphitic layer model and the cylindrical pore model-are constructed guided by small-angle X-ray scattering and transmission electron microscopy characterization.  The planar graphitic layers with interlayer distance &lt;6.5 Å are beneficial for metal storage, but do not have significant contribution to rapid metal diffusion. The cylindrical pore model, reflecting the curved morphol., does not increase metal storage, but significantly lowers the metal migration barriers. Such characterization represents an important step toward understanding and improving the properties and functionality of porous carbons, yet few exptl. techniques have been developed for this purpose.  Here we demonstrate the application of NMR spectroscopy and pair distribution function (PDF) anal. as new tools to probe the local structures of porous carbons, alongside more conventional Raman spectroscopy.  Together, the PDFs and the Raman spectra allow the local chem. bonding to be probed, with the bonding becoming more ordered for carbide-derived carbons (CDCs) synthesized at higher temps. Preferred values of the C-C bond length for stages 1 to 6, derived from the value of the a-axis parameter extrapolated to θ = 90°, are: Stage 1 1.43204 (3) A., Stage 2 1.42592 (3) A., Stage 3 1.4240 (1) A., Stage 4 1.42305 (7) A., Stage 5 1.42267 (6) A., and Stage 6 1.4221 (1) A. The bond-length expansion is consistent with electron transfer from K to the antibonding orbitals in the upper π-band of the graphitic region between successive intercalate layers.         </div><div class="casAbstract">There is no doubt that rechargeable batteries will play a huge role in the future of the world.  Sodium-ion (Na-ion) batteries might be the ideal middle-ground between high performance delivered by the modern lithium-ion (Li-ion) battery, desire for low costs and long-term sustainability.  To commercialise the Na-ion technol., Faradion was founded in 2011 as the world's first non-aq. Na-ion battery company.  Over the years, we have made rapid progress in increasing the all-around performance of Na-ion batteries, benefitting from decades' worth of industry experience and prior Na-ion as well as Li-ion academic research.  The Faradion Na-ion chem. can now exceed the energy densities of LiFePO4//graphite Li-ion batteries with rapidly converging cycle lives, similar rate performance and charge acceptance.  In addn., our technol. makes use of lower materials costs, offers improved safety through the use of high flash point electrolytes and has the ability to be discharged to zero volts for storage and transportation.  In this article, Faradion's step-by-step progress in the Na-ion technol. will be discussed together with a general picture of how our Na-ion chem. compares with other Na-ion systems and com. available Li-ion technol.  Finally, the importance of starting exptl. testing on new materials, keeping com.-relevant protocols in mind, will be illustrated by clearly highlighting the drastic effects of some crucial exptl. factors.  By sharing such industry know-how, Faradion hopes researchers worldwide will adopt such exptl. protocols as routine methodol. in the lab.     </div><div class="casAbstract">Synchrotron light source facilities worldwide generate terabytes of data in numerous incompatible data formats from a wide range of experiment types.  The Data Analysis WorkbeNch (DAWN) was developed to address the challenge of providing a single visualization and analysis platform for data from any synchrotron experiment (including single-crystal and powder diffraction, tomography and spectroscopy), whilst also being sufficiently extensible for new specific use case analysis environments to be incorporated (e.g.  ARPES, PEEM).  In this work, the history and current state of DAWN are presented, with two case studies to demonstrate specific functionality.  The first is an example of a data processing and reduction problem using the generic tools, whilst the second shows how these tools can be targeted to a specific scientific area.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref42"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref42" class="rightTabRefNumLink">42</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit42" data-doi="10.1107/S0021889804011744"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Qiu, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Thompson, J. W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Billinge, S. J. L.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">PDFgetX2 : A GUI-Driven Program to Obtain the Pair Distribution Function from X-Ray Powder Diffraction Data</span>. <i>J. Appl. Crystallogr.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2004</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>37</i></span> (<span class="NLM_issue">4</span>),  <span class="NLM_fpage">678</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1107/S0021889804011744</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit42R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">42</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">PDFgetX2: a GUI-driven program to obtain the pair distribution function from X-ray powder diffraction data</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Qiu, Xiangyun; Thompson, Jeroen W.; Billinge, Simon J. L.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Applied Crystallography</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2004</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">37</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">4</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">678</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JACGAR</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0021-8898</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Blackwell Publishing Ltd.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">
                There is no expanded citation for this reference.
            </div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref43"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref43" class="rightTabRefNumLink">43</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit43" data-doi="10.1107/S2053273315014473"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Juhás, P.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Farrow, C. L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yang, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Knox, K. R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Billinge, S. J. L.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Complex Modeling: A Strategy and Software Program for Combining Multiple Information Sources to Solve Ill Posed Structure and Nanostructure Inverse Problems</span>. <i>Acta Crystallogr., Sect. A: Found. Adv.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2015</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>71</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">562</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">568</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1107/S2053273315014473</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit43R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">43</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Complex modeling: a strategy and software program for combining multiple information sources to solve ill posed structure and nanostructure inverse problems</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Juhas Pavol; Knox Kevin R; Billinge Simon J L; Farrow Christopher L; Yang Xiaohao</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Acta crystallographica. Section A, Foundations and advances</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2015</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">71</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">Pt 6</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">562-8</span>
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn"></span>.
    </div><div class="casAbstract">A strategy is described for regularizing ill posed structure and nanostructure scattering inverse problems (i.e. structure solution) from complex material structures.  This paper describes both the philosophy and strategy of the approach, and a software implementation, DiffPy Complex Modeling Infrastructure (DiffPy-CMI).</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref44"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref44" class="rightTabRefNumLink">44</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit44" data-doi="10.1016/j.jmr.2016.02.008"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Pecher, O.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Bayley, P. M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Trease, N. M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Grey, C. P.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Automatic Tuning Matching Cycler (ATMC) in Situ NMR Spectroscopy as a Novel Approach for Real-Time Investigations of Li- and Na-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>J. Magn. Reson.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2016</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>265</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">200</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">209</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1016/j.jmr.2016.02.008</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit44R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">44</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Automatic Tuning Matching Cycler (ATMC) in situ NMR spectroscopy as a novel approach for real-time investigations of Li- and Na-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Pecher, Oliver; Bayley, Paul M.; Liu, Hao; Liu, Zigeng; Trease, Nicole M.; Grey, Clare P.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Magnetic Resonance</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2016</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">265</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">200-209</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JMARF3</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1090-7807</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier B.V.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">We have developed and explored the use of a new Automatic Tuning Matching Cycler (ATMC) in situ NMR probe system to track the formation of intermediate phases and investigate electrolyte decompn. during electrochem. cycling of Li- and Na-ion batteries (LIBs and NIBs).  The new approach addresses many of the issues arising during in situ NMR, e.g., significantly different shifts of the multi-component samples, changing sample conditions (such as the magnetic susceptibility and cond.) during cycling, signal broadening due to paramagnetism as well as interferences between the NMR and external cycler circuit that might impair the expts.  We provide practical insight into how to conduct ATMC in situ NMR expts. and discuss applications of the methodol. to LiFePO4 (LFP) and Na3V2(PO4)2F3 cathodes as well as Na metal anodes.  Automatic frequency sweep 7Li in situ NMR reveals significant changes of the strongly paramagnetic broadened LFP line shape in agreement with the structural changes due to delithiation.  Addnl., 31P in situ NMR shows a full sepn. of the electrolyte and cathode NMR signals and is a key feature for a deeper understanding of the processes occurring during charge/discharge on the local at. scale of NMR. 31P in situ NMR with "on-the-fly" re-calibrated, varying carrier frequencies on Na3V2(PO4)2F3 as a cathode in a NIB enabled the detection of different P signals within a huge frequency range of 4000 ppm.  The expts. show a significant shift and changes in the no. as well as intensities of 31P signals during desodiationc/sodiation of the cathode.  The in situ expts. reveal changes of local P environments that in part have not been seen in ex situ NMR investigations.  Furthermore, we applied ATMC 23Na in situ NMR on sym. Na-Na cells during galvanostatic plating.  An automatic adjustment of the NMR carrier frequency during the in situ expt. ensured on-resonance conditions for the Na metal and electrolyte peak, resp.  Thus, interleaved measurements with different optimal NMR set-ups for the metal and electrolyte, resp., became possible.  This allowed the formation of different Na metal species as well as a quantification of electrolyte consumption during the electrochem. expt. to be monitored.  The new approach is likely to benefit a further understanding of Na-ion battery chemistries.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref45"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref45" class="rightTabRefNumLink">45</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit45" data-doi="10.1006/jmre.2002.2554"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Harris, R. K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Becker, E. D.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">NMR Nomenclature: Nuclear Spin Properties and Conventions for Chemical Shifts—IUPAC Recommendations</span>. <i>J. Magn. Reson.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2002</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>156</i></span> (<span class="NLM_issue">2</span>),  <span class="NLM_fpage">323</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">326</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1006/jmre.2002.2554</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit45R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">45</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">NMR nomenclature: nuclear spin properties and conventions for chemical shifts - IUPAC recommendations</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Harris, Robin K.; Becker, Edwin D.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Magnetic Resonance</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2002</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">156</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">2</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">323-326</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JMARF3</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1090-7807</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier Science</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The IUPAC recommendations on nuclear spin properties and conventions for chem. shifts are updated.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li></ol></li><li id="pane-m3" aria-labelledby="pane-m3" class="tab__pane tab__pane-suppInfo"><h2 class="supp-info-label">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article_supporting-info" id="rightTab-notes-1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="rightTab-_i22">Supporting Information</h2></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge at <a href="/doi/10.1021/jacs.1c06058?goto=supporting-info" class="ext-link">https://pubs.acs.org/doi/10.1021/jacs.1c06058</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">Small-angle scattering data, elemental analysis and total scattering data for the carbons; additional details of the PDF processing and calculation methods; and additional PDF calculations (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/jacs.1c06058/suppl_file/ja1c06058_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">PDF</a>)</p></li></ul><p></p><div class="supInfoBoxOnFTP "><div class="sup-info-attachments"><ul><li><a class="suppl-anchor" href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/jacs.1c06058/suppl_file/ja1c06058_si_001.pdf">ja1c06058_si_001.pdf (3.15 MB)</a></li></ul></div></div><div id="rightTab-figshare-widget"></div><br><div class="TermsAndConditionsDropzoneRight" data-pb-dropzone="TermsAndConditionsDropzoneRight"><div data-widget-def="UX3HTMLWidget" data-widget-id="b84e6b6c-b4d0-4427-b286-3cf14d52d013" class="mb-4">

        <script nonce="9318ae004b1288d3-SJC">
(function(){
	
	/*Event Tracking for RIS menu dropdown - Article pages*/

	$('.cit-download-dropdown_button').on('click',function(e)
	{	
		e.preventDefault();
	    gtag('event', 'Export', { 'event_category': 'Citation', 'event_label':'Main'});

	});


	$('.cit-download-dropdown_content ul li').on('click',function(e)
	{	
	//	e.preventDefault();
	    var idx = $(this).index();
	    
	    var label = '';
	    if (idx === 0 ) {
	    	label = 'Citation';
	    } else if (idx === 1 ) {
	    	label = 'Abstract';
	    } else if (idx === 2 ) {
	    	label = 'Reference';
	    } else if (idx === 3 ) {
	    	label = 'More Options';
	    }
	   
	   gtag('event', 'Export', { 'event_category': 'Citation', 'event_label':label});
	});

	$('.header_contnav-next a').on('click', function(e){
        e.preventDefault();
        gtag('event', 'Top', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Right Arrow'});
	}); 

 	$('.header_contnav-pre a').on('click', function(e){
	    e.preventDefault();
	     gtag('event', 'Top', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Left Arrow'});
	}); 

	$('.share_button').on('click', function(e){
		e.preventDefault();
		gtag('event', 'Top', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Share'});
	});

	$("#pane-m1con").on('click', function(e) {
		e.preventDefault();
		gtag('event', 'Sidebar', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Figures'});
	});

	$("#pane-m2con").on('click', function(e) {
		e.preventDefault();
	    gtag('event', 'Sidebar', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'References'});
	});

	$("#pane-m3con").on('click', function(e) {
		e.preventDefault();
		gtag('event', 'Sidebar', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Supporting Info'});
	});

	$('.tab__close').on('click', function(){
		gtag('event', 'Sidebar', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Close'});
	}); 



})();

</script>

<script nonce="9318ae004b1288d3-SJC">
document.addEventListener('DOMContentLoaded', function () {
    const modalContainer = document.querySelector('.my-main-container');

    // Function to show the modal
    function showModal() {
        modalContainer.style.display = 'flex';
        document.querySelector('.overlay').style.display = 'block';
    }

    // Function to hide the modal when the close button is clicked
    function hideModal() {
        modalContainer.style.display = 'none';
        document.querySelector('.overlay').style.display = 'none';
    }

    // Hide the modal initially
    hideModal();

    // Function to handle clicks outside the modal
    function handleClickOutside(event) {
        // const modalContainer = document.querySelector(".my-main-container");
        // Check if the click target is not a child of the modal container
        if (!modalContainer.contains(event.target)) {
            hideModal();
        }
    }

    // Add a click event listener to the close button
    document
        .querySelector('.close-button')
        .addEventListener('click', hideModal);

    // Add a click event listener to the document to handle clicks outside the modal
    document.addEventListener('click', handleClickOutside);

    const screenHeight = window.innerHeight;
    function updateModalPosition() {
        const scrollY = window.scrollY;
        // console.log("scrollY= " + scrollY);
        // modalContainer.style.top = `${50 + scrollY}px`;
        modalContainer.style.top = `${scrollY + screenHeight / 2}px`;
        // console.log("new top= " + modalContainer.style.top);
    }

    // Update the modal position when the user scrolls
    window.addEventListener('scroll', updateModalPosition);

    // Initialize the modal position
    updateModalPosition();

    //---Cookie---
    // Function to set the cookie with a given name, value, and expiration time in days
    function setCookie(name, value, days) {
        var expires = '';
        if (days) {
            var date = new Date();
            date.setTime(date.getTime() + days * 24 * 60 * 60 * 1000);
            expires = '; expires=' + date.toUTCString();
            console.log(expires + 'this is expires dateUTC');
        }
        document.cookie = name + '=' + (value || '') + expires + '; path=/';
        // console.log("document.cookie is: " + document.cookie);
    }

    if (!document.cookie.includes('modalAd')) {
        // Show Modal
        showModal();

        // Set the cookie with the name "modalAd", value "true", and expiration of 3 day
        setCookie('modalAd', 'true', 3);
    } else {
        hideModal();
    }
});

</script>

<script nonce="9318ae004b1288d3-SJC">
    document.addEventListener("DOMContentLoaded", function(){
    // Function to get a Human readable formatted date
    function getFormattedDateForMaintenance() {
        let today = new Date();
        let dd = String(today.getDate()).padStart(2, '0');
        //January is 0
        let mm = String(today.getMonth() + 1).padStart(2, '0'); 
        let yyyy = today.getFullYear();
        
        let daysOfWeek = ['Sunday', 'Monday', 'Tuesday', 'Wednesday', 'Thursday', 'Friday', 'Saturday'];
        let monthsOfYear = ['January', 'February', 'March', 'April', 'May', 'June', 'July', 'August', 'September', 'October', 'November', 'December'];

        let dayOfWeek = daysOfWeek[today.getDay()];
        let monthOfYear = monthsOfYear[today.getMonth()];

        return dayOfWeek + ', ' + monthOfYear + ' ' + dd + ', ' + yyyy;
    }
    
    // Insert the date into the .system-message-date element
    document.querySelector('.system-message-date').innerText = getFormattedDateForMaintenance();
});
</script>

      function init() {
        setTimeout(function () {
          var cookieExist = getCookie("gdpr");
          if (cookieExist) {
            //console.log("gdpr cookie exist");
            return false;
          } else {
            //console.log("gdpr cookie does not exist")
            gdprMessage();
          }
          //Events
          $("#closebtn").on("click", hideForm);

          //Handle submit
          $("#gdpr-message-btn").on("click", function (event) {
            handleClick();
          });

          // //Hide if clicked outside div - TO DO stop handling click after submit
          // $(document).click(function(event) {
          // 	event.stopPropagation();

            setCookie("gdpr", "true");

          });

      //Show the message
      function gdprMessage() {
        //console.log("Show GDPR Message");
        var tMessage, emailInput, messageButton, ewrap;

        $("#gdpr-message-modal").show();
      }

      function handleClick() {
        //console.log($("#gdpr-input").val());
        emailValid = validateEmail($("#gdpr-input").val());
        if (emailValid) {
          //Submit the form
          //Hide form
          if ($(".gdpr_err")) {
            $(".gdpr_err").hide();
          }

          $("#gdpr-message-content");
          //If successful then...
          setCookie("gdpr", "true");
          //console.log("emailValid " + emailValid + " submiting form");
        } else {
          //Show error message
          gdprErrorMessage();
          //console.log("emailValid " + emailValid);
        }
      }

      function gdprErrorMessage() {
        if (errorStatus) {
          return false;
        } else {
          errorStatus = true;
          $("#gdpr-message-content").append(errormessage);
        }
      }

      function validateEmail(email) {
        var re =
          /^(([^<>()\[\]\\.,;:\s@"]+(\.[^<>()\[\]\\.,;:\s@"]+)*)|(".+"))@((\[[0-9]{1,3}\.[0-9]{1,3}\.[0-9]{1,3}\.[0-9]{1,3}\])|(([a-zA-Z\-0-9]+\.)+[a-zA-Z]{2,}))$/;
        return re.test(String(email).toLowerCase());
      }

      function setCookie(name, value) {
        var expires = "";
        expires = "; expires=Fri, 31 Dec 9999 23:59:59 GMT";
        document.cookie = name + "=" + (value || "") + expires + "; path=/";
      }

      function getCookie(name) {
        var match = document.cookie.match(new RegExp(name + "=([^;]+)"));
        if (match) return match[1];
      }

      function hideForm() {
        //And set cookie
        $("#gdpr-message-modal").hide();
        setCookie("gdpr", "false");
        gtag("event", "GDPR Modal", {
          event_category: "Pubs GDPR",
          event_label: "GDPR Dismissal",
        });
      }

      return {
        init: init,
      };
    })();

var currentJournal = "Journal of the American Chemical Society";
$(".citationLinks > a").click(function(e){
    if($(this).text() === "ACS Full Text ") {
        gtag('event', 'ACS Full Text', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).text() === "Crossref") {
        gtag('event', 'Crossref', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).text() === "CAS") {
        gtag('event', 'CAS', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).text() === "PubMed") {
        gtag('event', 'PubMed', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).text() === "Google Scholar") {
        gtag('event', 'Google Scholar', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).hasClass("issue-item_open-url") === true) {
        gtag('event', 'OpenURL', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
});
